  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_bebca098-e430-4a70-a27b-8c8260e08e58'
      });

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2485263880" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2485263880" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7365405-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522094190p2/7365405.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2485263880?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/7365405-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7365405-james?shelf=3-multi-book-series">3-multi-book-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7365405-james?shelf=2-fic-childrens">2-fic-childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7365405-james?shelf=1-fiction">1-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7365405-james?shelf=9-read-2018-08-august">9-read-2018-08-august</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2485263880" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15601120275077628628">As part of a children's book readathon on <a href='https://thisismytruthnow.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>my blog</a><a></a> in August 2018, voters chose <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99107.Winnie_the_Pooh__Winnie_the_Pooh___1_' title='Winnie-the-Pooh (Winnie-the-Pooh, #1) by A.A. Milne' rel='nofollow noopener'>Winnie-the-Pooh</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/81466.A_A__Milne' title='A.A. Milne' rel='nofollow noopener'>A.A. Milne</a> as one of our award-winning books to read this week. I was very excited to pick up this classic again as I haven't read it in over thirty years. I've seen several shows and cartoons with nieces, nephews, and cousins, but reading the wonderfully illustrated picture books was a fresh experience. I adore the world Milne has created with all the amazing characters in the woods. It's a bit of </span>
  <span id="freeText15601120275077628628" style="display:none">As part of a children's book readathon on <a href="https://thisismytruthnow.com" rel="nofollow noopener">my blog</a><a></a> in August 2018, voters chose <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99107.Winnie_the_Pooh__Winnie_the_Pooh___1_" title="Winnie-the-Pooh (Winnie-the-Pooh, #1) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">Winnie-the-Pooh</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/81466.A_A__Milne" title="A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">A.A. Milne</a> as one of our award-winning books to read this week. I was very excited to pick up this classic again as I haven't read it in over thirty years. I've seen several shows and cartoons with nieces, nephews, and cousins, but reading the wonderfully illustrated picture books was a fresh experience. I adore the world Milne has created with all the amazing characters in the woods. It's a bit of fantasy and magic combined with reality and lessons. I'd forgotten how 'mischievous' Pooh and his friends could be.<br /><br />When we picked this classic, I never specified which of the books to read -- silly of me, actually, as Pooh might think or say! I left it up to each reader. I borrowed the book from the library, and it seems to be books 1 and 2, so I read more than I'd planned. At about 150 pages, it has some illustrations but way more text than I remembered. It was still adorable to read. There were ~10 stories introducing different characters and scenarios ranging from an encounter with bumble bees (where Pooh classically steals the honey -- or tries to!) to poor Eeyore losing his tail.<br /><br />If you've never sampled Winnie the Pooh, get to the library now and read one of the stories. At the very least, tempt yourself by watching a cartoon version. At least now I know where Christopher Robin comes from... who the bear family is and what a pooh actually means! I definitely want to check out the movie that came out earlier this year (last year?) on Christopher Robin!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15601120275077628628" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_684769861" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/684769861" />
    <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma"><img alt="Nandakishore Varma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583648733p2/6237864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/684769861?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nandakishore Varma" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma">Nandakishore Varma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780603562136" href="/book/show/1781288.The_Complete_Winnie_the_Pooh">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer684769861" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18229027426701245720"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380431627i/866241._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Have a deep, long look at the image above. That motley crew are undoubtedly the most famous toy animals in existence.<br/><br/>Winnie-the-Pooh, Piglet, Eeyore, Kanga (I cannot see Roo) and (last but not least) Tigger.<br/><br/>A. A. Milne, and established playwright and writer, constructed silly nursery stories and poems for his young son Christopher Robin, built around his beloved toys. He published them. And much to his chagrin, he came to be known as the creator of "Winnie-the-Pooh": all his "serious" works were</span>
  <span id="freeText18229027426701245720" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380431627i/866241._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Have a deep, long look at the image above. That motley crew are undoubtedly the most famous toy animals in existence.<br><br>Winnie-the-Pooh, Piglet, Eeyore, Kanga (I cannot see Roo) and (last but not least) Tigger.<br><br>A. A. Milne, and established playwright and writer, constructed silly nursery stories and poems for his young son Christopher Robin, built around his beloved toys. He published them. And much to his chagrin, he came to be known as the creator of "Winnie-the-Pooh": all his "serious" works were forgotten!<br><br>Read this book, and you will understand why.<br><br>True, nothing much happens in the stories. There are no hair-raising escapades, no dashing adventures and no earth-shaking events. What we have here are a bunch of rather silly animals (the team mentioned above, along with two imaginary ones, Rabbit and Owl) in Hundred Acre Woods, doing a lot of silly things, talking nonsensically (though pompously) most of the time, and making prize fools of themselves. Yet these stories are magical, for adults and children alike.<br><br>Christopher Robin is the acknowledged lord of this idyllic kingdom: the stories start when he comes down the stairs, dragging Pooh-bear behind him ("bump, bump, bump") and ends when he goes up the stairs in the same fashion. The cosy world of the nursery transforms itself into a magic land where you can hunt "heffalumps" or go on "expotitions" to the North Pole. The cast of characters are always the same, and the happenings, similar. Where these stories score are in the way the characters are etched. With true English underplayed humour, Milne has invested these stuffed toys with fascinating personalities.<br><br>Pooh, the "Bear of Very Little Brain", but subject to occasional flashes of brilliance and bursts of versification.<br><br>Piglet, the smallest and weakest of them all but sometimes capable of doing "Very Grand Things".<br><br>The clever Rabbit, many a time too much so for his own good.<br><br>The pedantic and pompous Owl, who can't restrain himself from holding forth at the slightest provocation.<br><br>The long-suffering Eeyore with his never-ending complaints.<br><br>The devoted Kanga and her frisky little son Roo, whom she keeps in her pocket.<br><br>Happy-go-lucky Tigger, bouncing all over the woods.<br><br>These characters are typically English: in fact, they could have stepped out from a P.G.Wodehouse novel. When a child reads these stories, he/ she will enjoy them at their face value; while the perceptive adult will be fascinated by the subtext.<br><br>It is no surprise that these stories endure. As Milne says: "...the Forest will always be there...and anybody who is Friendly with Bears can find it." Christopher Robin will grow up; making way for other kids who will take his place. But this imaginary landscape will endure, because "in that enchanted place on the top of the Forest a little boy and his Bear will always be playing."<br><br>Fantastic book!<br><br>P.S. The illustrations by E.H.Shepard should also be mentioned. They are so much a part of the story that we cannot imagine the book without the pictures.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18229027426701245720" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/217628618" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/217628618?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780603550836" href="/book/show/1899581.The_Complete_Winnie_the_Pooh">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=well-i-think-its-funny">well-i-think-its-funny</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=celebrity-death-match">celebrity-death-match</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=parody-homage">parody-homage</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=children">children</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1713956-manny?shelf=why-not-call-it-poetry">why-not-call-it-poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer217628618" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11608158372085523994"><b>For <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/277496?chapter=3' rel='nofollow noopener'>the Celebrity Death Match Review Tournament</a>, <i>Heart of Darkness</i> (25) versus <i>The Complete Tales and Poems of Winnie-the-Pooh</i> (24)</b><br/><br/><b>In which the animals go on a Second Expotition, and Pooh discovers that Not Everyone Likes Hums</b><br/><br/>There was a corner of the Hundred Acre Wood that the animals rarely visited. Even Eeyore found it too Sad and Gloomy, and it had more than its fair share of annoying insects. Owl, in his grand way, sometimes called it the Forest's Heart of Darkness, and that always made Pig</span>
  <span id="freeText11608158372085523994" style="display:none"><b>For <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/277496?chapter=3" rel="nofollow noopener">the Celebrity Death Match Review Tournament</a>, <i>Heart of Darkness</i> (25) versus <i>The Complete Tales and Poems of Winnie-the-Pooh</i> (24)</b><br /><br /><b>In which the animals go on a Second Expotition, and Pooh discovers that Not Everyone Likes Hums</b><br /><br />There was a corner of the Hundred Acre Wood that the animals rarely visited. Even Eeyore found it too Sad and Gloomy, and it had more than its fair share of annoying insects. Owl, in his grand way, sometimes called it the Forest's Heart of Darkness, and that always made Piglet shiver and say, thank goodness, <i>he</i> wasn't going to go there soon, no thank you! So as you can imagine, not all the animals were pleased when Christopher Robin told them they would undertake a Second Expotition to find out what was in the Dark Patch.<br /><br />"I'm not going there, no thank you!" said Piglet, trying to sound as firm as possible. "I'm very busy, any number of things to do, like, like..." But Christopher Robin just laughed.<br /><br />"Don't worry, Piglet!" he said. "We'll all look after you. Just stay next to Pooh and you'll be <i>quite</i> safe." And before Piglet knew what had happened, they were all walking towards the Dark Patch in a long line, with Christopher Robin and Pooh and him at the front, Rabbit's Friends and Relations at the end, and the other animals in the middle.<br /><br />The Dark Patch was even Darker and Gloomier than they remembered, and strange noises came from the trees. The further in they got, the worse it became. The ground turned wet and marshy, and one Friend and Relation had to be pulled out when he started to sink. Piglet clutched Pooh's hand as tightly as he could and tried not to look around. <br /><br />"I'm scared, Pooh," he whispered. "You don't think there are Heffalumps here?"<br /><br />"What <i>I</i> think," said Christoper Robin, who had overheard, "is that Pooh should give us one of his Hums." And Pooh, who had been thinking the very same thing but had been too shy to say so, cleared his throat and began:<blockquote>
  <i>On Monday, when the jungle's hot<br />I wonder to myself a lot<br />Now is it true or is it not<br />That what is which or which is what?</i>
</blockquote>Piglet released his grip on Pooh's hand a tiny fraction, so he continued.<blockquote>
  <i>On Tuesday, when there's gnats and fleas<br />And pythons slither through the trees<br />Then very readily one sees<br />That these are whose - but whose are these?</i>
</blockquote>"There aren't <i>really</i> any Pythons?" asked Piglet in a terrified voice.<br /><br />"Well," said Pooh, "I only put them in because they Came To Me. I'm going to take them right out again." And he continued<blockquote>
  <i>On Wednesday...</i>
</blockquote>But the animals never found out what happened on Wednesday, because at that moment a loud, groaning voice came from the forest right in front of them.<br /><br />"The Hummer! The Hummer!" it said.<br /><br />"Oh Pooh!" said Piglet. "It <i>is</i> a Python! Or a Heffalump! Oh, what shall we do!" <br /><br />"I don't know," said Pooh. "Whatever it is, it Doesn't Like My Hums." He wondered if he should feel offended, but before he could decide they suddenly came out in a remarkably pleasant clearing. The sun was shining brightly, there was soft grass to sit on, butterflies were flitting between the flowers, and a charming little lake just seemed to call out to the animals to paddle their tired feet in it.<br /><br />"What a lovely place!" said Kanga in surprise. "Who could have imagined it would be right in the middle of the Dark Patch?"<br /><br />"I shall call it Pooh's Pond," said Christopher Robin firmly. "And <i>now</i> I think it's time for lunch."<br /><br />So they all unpacked their food and had a perfectly wonderful picnic. And from that day on, no one was ever again scared of the Dark Part of the Forest.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11608158372085523994" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_223425963" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/223425963" />
    <a title="Mariel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/647547-mariel"><img alt="Mariel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451321467p2/647547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/223425963?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mariel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mariel" href="/user/show/647547-mariel">Mariel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/858534.The_Complete_Tales_of_Winnie_the_Pooh">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Lassie</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mariel by: </span>
      The Magdalene Laundries
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer223425963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8428451929876684344"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/277496-celebrity-death-match-review-elimination-tournament?chapter=4' rel='nofollow noopener'>Celebrity Death Match</a> tournament versus Mary Poppins.<br/>Christopher Robin: May I color with my Winnie the Pooh and friends coloring book before I make up my bed with ideal hospital corners? My shins are scraped from having too much fun cleaning.<br/>His boy lips turn to blue in his deathly pale white face. Her mask conforms to a perfect Noh shape. He reads the lips. No. Oh noh! Oh no! Christopher Robin is dying.<br/>Mary Poppins: I am Governess to the good Christopher Robin. My credentials say it all because</span>
  <span id="freeText8428451929876684344" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/277496-celebrity-death-match-review-elimination-tournament?chapter=4" rel="nofollow noopener">Celebrity Death Match</a> tournament versus Mary Poppins.<br>Christopher Robin: May I color with my Winnie the Pooh and friends coloring book before I make up my bed with ideal hospital corners? My shins are scraped from having too much fun cleaning.<br>His boy lips turn to blue in his deathly pale white face. Her mask conforms to a perfect Noh shape. He reads the lips. No. Oh noh! Oh no! Christopher Robin is dying.<br>Mary Poppins: I am Governess to the good Christopher Robin. My credentials say it all because I do it all: I transform children into productive adults of society who will feel a fondness for me long after they have toppled their mothers and fathers from their pedestals. That is to say that my shit never stinks. But today he is too ill to tidy up the nursery. Another name he asks to see on his dying breath. A spoonful of fun fun coated in chocolate frosting has not made the medicine go down in the most delicious way. Mary! Not the virgin Mary. It is I, Mary Poppins. I suspect the bear has sewn the pills into his belly and is redistributing them on the black market, to the hospital orderlies and registered nurses, to the children's ward and all, for a tidy profit. I have laughed until I have cried to the ceiling and back. Now I ask you, O wise one, what should I do to reach this boy who would have a higher calling than to listen to me? Deathliness is closer to godliness, I fear, more than cleanliness.<br>The doctor: I could make the bear more money on his fun fun, now that you mention it.<br>Mary Poppins conceals her reactions to this business opportunity behind her black umbrella. It is not savvy to look too eager. The dance of business. Joyless steps. It is a one way street. It takes two to tango and it takes one to screw over.<br>Mary Poppins: I make fun fun out of my beautiful spirit and endless bag lady bag of pilfered hospital toiletries and I have no use of middle men.<br>The doctor: I did not have enough to wipe today. I did not appreciate that.<br>Mary Poppins: It is important to wipe. Do you remember the proper wiping method that I had showed you when you were a tyke yourself?<br>The doctor's mask is less than a pristine white, now that Mary Poppins notices it. He should always need her. He would be a starving street artist if it were not for her, not to mention the laundry bills and streaked underpants.<br>Christopher Robin's spirit: I must get a message to my beloved menagerie. The foul governess has put them through the wash to rid them of "germs". My spirit grows weary of hovering between death, and between life in my smothered human body. The smell of Johnson &amp; Johnson blurs the lines between that of the spirit, and that of the Earth. It is how she wishes it.<br>Mary Poppins: Visiting hours are over.<br>She attempts to block that door with that versatile umbrella.<br>Winnie the Pooh: I step on the words of "Visiting". My tushie stays planted on "Hours" and time will not end until I have had my say.<br>Mary Poppins: Very well, but have you washed your hands? Let me see them.<br>Winnie's paws are covered with honey. He sticks them behind his plushy bottom.<br>Piglet: You... you! You cannooot-- stop- us! We have name badges. Seee--see? Visitor: Piglet.<br>The doctor looks over their visitor's badges and nods his approval. He motions to Winnie the Pooh the international sign of big bucks to be made. Pooh blushes.<br>Winnie: Christopher Robin, what are we to do without you? I have been turning more and more to the pot.<br>Christopher Robin's spirit bends down to kiss his bear on the forehead.<br>Winnie: We must go with other boys and girls and provide companionship. We may carry dust mites and illegal narcotics but we ground boys and girls in their beds at night, safe in day dreams and off of high ceilings. We do not use them to turn a profit for Mary Poppins Tidy the Nursery Cleaning Company.<br>Piglet: Am I crying? My comforting face appears the same. I... I.... would take to my small and big enough heart the feelings my friends cannot Pooh Bear alone.<br>The doctor: I am crying. That may be the high, though. If I had not given up the accordion I would bend my body in sad and joyous music. To Christopher Robin! He was me, the better me.<br>Mary Poppins: Visiting hours are over, I see.<br>Mary Poppins opens her umbrella over her inexpressive yet mournful face, giving seven years bad luck to all, and levitates out the window of the children's ward, slowly enough to read billboard advertisements for a new position. A woman learns an important life lesson when she first enters the dating scene: It is possible, although not rewarding enough to outweigh the costs, to come between a boy and his mother. Man's best friend, on the other hand...<br>Winnie: I am lonely.<br>The doctor scoops the animals up into his arms and they stop by surgery for some emergency sewing on Pooh's bulging behind. He will always need them.<br>Cast of Characters:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380361268i/712214.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Winnie Poh.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380361268i/712215.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Mary Oh No Means Noh.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380361268i/712216.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Yo Doc is Wack!<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380361268i/712217.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>BFF 4-ever!<br><b>Win: Winnie the Pooh</b></span>

<div id="review_2271590129" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2271590129" />
    <a title="Melora" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5586131-melora"><img alt="Melora" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548110965p2/5586131.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2271590129?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melora" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melora" href="/user/show/5586131-melora">Melora</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780416610505" href="/book/show/2193649.The_World_of_Pooh">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5586131-melora?shelf=children-s-books">children-s-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2271590129" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4187845702845147628">Another one of those “imbibed with mother's milk” books, like <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5659.The_Wind_in_the_Willows' title='The Wind in the Willows by Kenneth Grahame' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Wind in the Willows</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5907.The_Hobbit' title='The Hobbit by J.R.R. Tolkien' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Hobbit</a>, which I am incapable of commenting on with any sort of objectivity. I get a kick out of Pooh's “hums,” and the characters are old friends. My dad's nickname for my mom was “Pooh,” and she introduced him to the Pooh books when they were dating (he was a Jewish boy from Staten Island, and knew all about science and philosophy, but had missed out on most of the children's classics), and lines and c</span>
  <span id="freeText4187845702845147628" style="display:none">Another one of those “imbibed with mother's milk” books, like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5659.The_Wind_in_the_Willows" title="The Wind in the Willows by Kenneth Grahame" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wind in the Willows</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5907.The_Hobbit" title="The Hobbit by J.R.R. Tolkien" rel="nofollow noopener">The Hobbit</a>, which I am incapable of commenting on with any sort of objectivity. I get a kick out of Pooh's “hums,” and the characters are old friends. My dad's nickname for my mom was “Pooh,” and she introduced him to the Pooh books when they were dating (he was a Jewish boy from Staten Island, and knew all about science and philosophy, but had missed out on most of the children's classics), and lines and characters from the books were part of our family culture. My copy is one I purchased on a long-ago and fondly remembered trip to England with my best childhood friend, so there's that much <i>more</i> sentiment involved. You get the picture – I turn to mush when it comes to Pooh. Anyway, this is my last “read this to my mom while she was dying of dementia/cancer” review, because, well, I don't get to read her any more books. <i>But</i>, we got to finish this one. On Tuesday morning, her last day, I read her chapter IX, which is the one where Eeyore finds Owl a new house, only it happens to be Piglet's house, and Piglet does the Noble thing, and then X, “An Enchanted Place.” That last chapter chokes me up in a “normal” reading – when reading to a child. Christopher Robin is leaving his “nursery days,” and he asks Pooh to always remember him. He, Christopher Robin, doesn't <i>want</i> to leave, but it's time and he has to, but a part of him will always remain in this enchanted place with Pooh. Mom was fading away, but she was still aware and registered the illustrations I showed her (I only bothered her with the best ones). She passed away Tuesday night, and I'll miss her terribly, but I think this was a fine book to end with.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4187845702845147628" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1515490971" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1515490971" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1515490971?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/858534.The_Complete_Tales_of_Winnie_the_Pooh">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1515490971" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13194896021044383294">I think everyone has heard the name Winnie-the-Pooh at some point. I even remember watching the TV show when I was younger (but not for long). <br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454241550i/17934430._SX540_.jpg' width='400' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454241550i/17934431._SX540_.jpg' width='400' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>However, I'm one of those people who never got to read the book as a kid and I only rediscovered it now while catching up with all the classics I've missed out on.<br/>So I found this all-in-one volume online and had to have it since it not only contains all the stories but also has the original illustrations by Ernest Howard Shepard.<br/><br/>I have to say, this book i</span>
  <span id="freeText13194896021044383294" style="display:none">I think everyone has heard the name Winnie-the-Pooh at some point. I even remember watching the TV show when I was younger (but not for long). <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454241550i/17934430._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454241550i/17934431._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>However, I'm one of those people who never got to read the book as a kid and I only rediscovered it now while catching up with all the classics I've missed out on.<br>So I found this all-in-one volume online and had to have it since it not only contains all the stories but also has the original illustrations by Ernest Howard Shepard.<br><br>I have to say, this book is especially delightful because it contains lots of information about the author and illustrator and I always love learning about the interconnectedness of the literary world.<br><br>In this case, we learn that the author A.A. Milne had a son called Christopher Robin who was his inspiration. <br>His son's first toys were a stuffed bear, pig, donkey and tiger - the animals that formed this story. The name for the bear, in part, comes from a she-bear that was kept at London Zoo that Christopher Robin loved to visit (there is a great movie about it called <b>A Bear Named Winnie</b> with Michael Fassbender - I love it although I always cry)! And the story was only ever published thanks to Mrs. Milne! <br>We also learn that E.H. Shepard's daughter Mary is the illustrator of the <i>Mary Poppins</i> books and that E.H. Shepard became so famous for his Winnie Pooh illustrations that he was commissioned to do the original ones for <i>The Wind in the Willows</i> as well!<br>In fact, A.A. Milne loved the illustrations so much that he wrote the following tribute to E.H. Shepard:<br><blockquote>
  <i>When I am gone<br>Let Shepard decorate my tomb,<br>And put (if there is room)<br>Two pictures on the stone;<br>Piglet from page a hundred and eleven<br>And Pooh and Piglet walking (157) ...<br>And Peter, thinking that they are my own,<br>Will welcome me to heaven.</i>
</blockquote><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454241550i/17934432.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The book itself is divided into two parts:<br>1) Winnie-the-Pooh, containing the Introduction by the author and 10 stories<br>and<br>2) The House At Pooh Corner, consisting of A Contraditction and 10 more stories.<br><br>Before the Introduction, there is another dedication, this time to Mrs. Milne:<br><blockquote>To Her<br>Hand in hand we come<br>Christopher Robin and I<br>To lay this book in your lap.<br>Say you're surprised?<br>Say you like it?<br>Say it's just what you wanted?<br>Because it's yours - <br>Because we love you.</blockquote><br><br>It's these little things that make this edition so special and precious because it allows us a glimpse at who the author was. To think that both A.A. Milne and E.H. Shepard served in World War I (and Shepard losing his first-born, a son, in Word War II) and yet managed to still be kind and gentle and creative and inspiring ... to me they radiate a certain brightness I did not expect from survivors of a World War.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454241550i/17934433.gif" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br>I don't think there is much I need to say about the stories themselves. <br>Pooh is the "silly old bear" who often gets into trouble or is just clumsy, while being surrounded by a marvellous cast of friends that are all distinct and lovely and funny. <br>The stories are heart-warming but also have a deeper meaning as one can only unlock while growing up. That is what makes these tales so important - to get the stories read to you by a parent when you are little, enjoying the sillyness and coziness of it all; then keep reading or re-reading them when you get older and discover another layer and some hidden messages. <br>To me, these are timeless and absolutely essential.<br><br>And here are my two absolute favourites (although I could quote almost the entire book here):<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454241550i/17934434.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454241550i/17934435._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="13194896021044383294" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2706876001" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16192581686570777776"><b>“Rivers know this: there is no hurry.   <span id="freeText16192581686570777776" style="display:none"><b>“Rivers know this: there is no hurry. We shall get there some day.” </b><br /><br />So I made myself a large cup of tea with honey, got under blankets and read this favorite of mine (well, a favorite might a an understatement). That's the problem.”</b><br /><br />There are kids books, and then there are books for all ages. Books that are not only adorable, but profound that they make you laugh out loud whether you're 9, 19 or 29 (and beyond of course).<br /><br /><b>“Pooh," said Rabbit kindly, "you haven't any brain." <br />"I know," said Pooh humbly.” </b><br /><br /><i>*The edition I read wasn't this one. <span id="freeTextContainer736786099962085306">Includes <i>Winnie-the-Pooh</i> and <i>The House At Pooh Corner</i> (both of which have 10 chapters).<br/><br/>A lot funnier than I expected. Started reading this a few days before <a href='https://nationaldaycalendar.com/national-winnie-the-pooh-day-january-18/' rel='nofollow noopener'>National Winnie-the-Pooh Day</a> (Jan. 18—the birthday of A. A. Milne, b. 1882).<br/><br/>A little confusing for young children because of the point-of-view: The narrator (A. A. Milne) is telling a story <i>about</i> his telling a story to his son, Christopher Robin (see pp. 1–2, 87). The "About A. A. Milne" section at the beginning says that Christopher Robin M</span>
  <span id="freeText736786099962085306" style="display:none">Includes <i>Winnie-the-Pooh</i> and <i>The House At Pooh Corner</i> (both of which have 10 chapters).<br /><br />A lot funnier than I expected. Started reading this a few days before <a href="https://nationaldaycalendar.com/national-winnie-the-pooh-day-january-18/" rel="nofollow noopener">National Winnie-the-Pooh Day</a> (Jan. 18—the birthday of A. A. Milne, b. 1882).<br /><br />A little confusing for young children because of the point-of-view: The narrator (A. A. Milne) is telling a story <i>about</i> his telling a story to his son, Christopher Robin (see pp. 1–2, 87). The "About A. A. Milne" section at the beginning says that Christopher Robin Milne was Alan Alexander and Daphne Milne's only child. He received stuffed animals (bear, tiger, pig, donkey) from Harrods. Apparently, Daphne is the one who first had the idea of have Alan write stories about the animals. Ernest H. Shepard illustrated the books based on his visit to the family farm, which has pine trees, a stream, and a bridge. Shepherd also illustrated Kenneth Grahame's <i>The Wind in the Willows</i>. Milne died the year my dad was born (1956).<br /><br />A common theme is the anxiety characters have about not knowing answers and appearing ignorant or foolish. Pooh and Piglet will often pretend to understand when they actually do not. Pooh is often described as a Bear of Little Brain.<br /><br /><u>Winnie-the-Pooh</u><br />2: Winnie is typically a nickname for girls named Winifred (although Winston Churchill was known to friends as <a href="https://www.telegraph.co.uk/history/9451741/Winston-Churchill-Americas-enduring-love-for-Winnie-and-his-words.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Winnie</a>); the justification of the female nickname for a male toy bear is both technical and playful: the name of the bear is "Winnie-the-Pooh," not just Winnie; the <i>ther</i> in "Winnie-ther-Pooh" comes from <a href="http://dialectblog.com/2011/09/10/intrusive-r/" rel="nofollow noopener">the British practice of adding the R sound after a schwa</a><br />18: Pooh got his name (possibly) from blowing off a fly on his nose.<br />29: "[W]ould you read a Sustaining Book, such as would help and comfort a Wedged Bear in Great Tightness?" (See <a href="https://www.facebook.com/photo.php?fbid=10161271222905058&amp;set=a.325228340057&amp;type=1&amp;theater" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.)<br />72: "Bon-hommy" (bonhomie)<br />77–78: Pooh has a Rom. 7 moment (he wants to do the right thing, but he gives in to his appetites)<br />131: Piglet remembers a story from the past that helps him take action in the present<br />135: Kara, Kate, and I lol'ed at the "Honey!" part<br />143–44: Kate laughed at Owl's boring story that almost kills Piglet (probably a play on "imprisonment")<br /><br /><u>The House at Pooh Corner</u><br />197: "It is the best way to write poetry, letting things come" (Pooh).<br />211–12: Piglet's fascinating daydream in which he becomes the hero of his own story<br />217–18: Piglet's embarrassment makes him want to run away, but one encouraging word changes everything<br />Ch. 5 was pretty funny (Kate [age 8] and I laughed a lot) and sweet: Rabbit's self-importance, Owl's unwillingness to admit his ignorance, Pooh's obliviousness, Piglet's thoughtfulness, Eeyore's grumpiness (and disappointment that Rabbit already knew what he thought only he knew), Christopher Robin's improvement in his education (spelling better)<br />256: very creative way of describing the stream<br />259: Poohsticks<br /><a href="https://www.facebook.com/photo.php?fbid=10161628093990058&amp;set=a.10153570304705058&amp;type=1&amp;theater" rel="nofollow noopener">266</a>: "Pooh began to feel a little more comfortable [after Piglet's compliment], because when you are a Bear of Very Little Brain, and you Think of Things, you find sometimes that a Thing which seemed very Thingish inside you is quite different when it gets out into the open and has other people looking at it." [#writing]<br />277, 290–91: Rabbit wants to unbounce Tigger and make him a "Small and Sorry Tigger, and Oh-Rabbit-I-<i>am</i>-glad-to-see-you Tigger"; by the end, Rabbit (who had gotten lost, and was found by Tigger) is a "Small and Sorry Rabbit" who says, "Oh, Tigger, I <i>am</i> glad to see you"<br />310: Owl spells his name "wol," so a wolery is an owlery (abode for owls).<br />311: Pooh: "Poetry and Hums aren't things which you get, they're things which get <i>you</i>. And all you can do is to go where they can find you." (Cf. <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=86x-u-tz0MA" rel="nofollow noopener">this TED talk</a>.)<br />324: Piglet (perhaps spurred on by Pooh's song about how gallant Piglet was) does a very noble thing, and it's very touching.<br />330: Eeyore has a "please clap" moment<br />334–44: The final ten pages with Christopher Robin and Pooh in the enchanted place are sweet. [The real life Christopher Milne wrote an autobiography titled <i>The Enchanted Places</i>]</span>

<div id="review_231849545" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/231849545" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4235780-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/231849545?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/4235780-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/858534.The_Complete_Tales_of_Winnie_the_Pooh">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer231849545" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16413254640978596425"><b> This review is for the Celebrity Death Match Tournament - <i> Winnie-the-Pooh</i> versus <i> Hamlet. </i> </b><br/><br/><br/>One day when the weather was especially fine, Pooh and his friends were playing Pooh sticks. Pooh was thinking how nice it would be to have a playing-Pooh-sticks-with-your-friends-on-an-especially-nice-day sort of hum when...<br/><br/><i> "Hey nonny nonny..."<br/><br/>"Was that me?" </i> Pooh asked Christopher Robin. <i> "You see, I was just thinking..."<br/>"Silly old bear," </i> said Christopher Robin fondly. <i>"That wasn't you. It was her."</i><br/><br/>E</span>
  <span id="freeText16413254640978596425" style="display:none"><b> This review is for the Celebrity Death Match Tournament - <i> Winnie-the-Pooh</i> versus <i> Hamlet. </i> </b><br /><br /><br />One day when the weather was especially fine, Pooh and his friends were playing Pooh sticks. Pooh was thinking how nice it would be to have a playing-Pooh-sticks-with-your-friends-on-an-especially-nice-day sort of hum when...<br /><br /><i> "Hey nonny nonny..."<br /><br />"Was that me?" </i> Pooh asked Christopher Robin. <i> "You see, I was just thinking..."<br />"Silly old bear," </i> said Christopher Robin fondly. <i>"That wasn't you. It was her."</i><br /><br />Everyone looked over the side of the bridge where Christopher Robin was pointing and saw a young woman floating in the river, clutching a ragged bouquet of wildflowers and stringing together nonsense words in a sort of song.<br /><br /><i> "Hey nonny nonny tiddly pom..."<br /><br />"I shall have to remember that one," </i> thought Pooh. <br />As she floated out of sight, Christopher Robin said, <i>"Let's go on an expedition to find out where she came from."</i> <br />So they did.<br /><br />Following the river, they reached the edge of the Hundred Acre Wood at about half-past teatime and were surprised to find out they were in Denmark.<br /><i>"Oh d-d-dear,"</i> worried Piglet, <i>"Are there any horrible creatures in D-d-d-enmark?"<br />"Let's go ask at that castle," </i> said Christopher Robin.<br />So they did.<br /><br />The path to the castle took them through a graveyard. Clods of earth were being flung furiously out of a new hole being dug. Suddenly, they all heard a whistling sort of voice ask,<br /><i>"Sssay, hasss anybody ssseen a ssskull around here?"</i><br />Up popped a gopher from the grave. He pawed in the pile of dirt a bit, found the skull he was looking for, and held it up.<br /><i> "Sssee thisss ssskull?"</i> he asked. <i>"It'sss..."<br />"Oh hush,"</i> said Christopher Robin. <i>"You aren't in the books at all. You were just an attempt to pander to American audiences and we shan't bother with you."</i><br />So they didn't.<br /><br />Arriving at the castle, the first person they met was practicing with a sword, which was <i> very </i> exciting, and muttering, <i>"If it be now, 'tis not to come; if it be not to come, it will be now; if it be not now, yet it will come,"</i> which wasn't.<br /><br />This muttering swordsman noticed them and extended his hand.<br /><i> "The name's Hamlet, title character in the greatest work in all of literature. Oh, and a prince."</i><br /><br />Christopher Robin shook his hand and asked politely, <i> "Excuse me, but why is it the greatest work of literature?"<br />"Because the play embodies Shakespeare's profound knowledge of human nature in all its complexity. I myself am a striking portrait of melancholia. Why I was just wondering the other day, 'To be or not to be...' "<br />"Better not," </i> said Eeyore melancholically. <i> "It never works out."<br />"And Polonius," </i> Hamlet continued, <i>"whom I, umm, killed a little while ago...he was all 'You should do this and don't do that, time and place for everything...' "<br />"Sounds like an excellent sort of chap," </i> said Rabbit, <i>"Nothing like plans and rules and explanations to know what's what, I always say!"</i><br />Hamlet frowned, his brow furrowed in thought. <i>"But I am also a skillful portrait of mania. I'm especially proud of my antic disposition."<br />"Antic disposition?"</i> Tigger said bouncily, <i>"That's what tiggers do best!"</i><br />Realizing that his human nature argument was, perhaps, not quite as strong as he thought, Hamlet brought out his final devastating argument, one he saved for last because of its embarrassing nature. <br /><i>"Ummm, Mummy issues?"</i> he offered, blushing a little.<br />Just then, Kanga's pouch roiled alarmingly and a fully grown Roo popped his head out. <br /><i>"What did he say, Mama?"<br />"Nothing dear, no need to interupt your nap."</i><br /><br />Dejectedly, Hamlet stalked out of the room with sword in hand, muttering about taking action.<br /><i>"Heard that one before,"</i> one of the guards by the throne room door chuckled.<br /><i>"Let's go watch the swordfight,"</i> said Christopher Robin.<br />So they did.<br /><br />When it was all over, Christopher Robin was cowering in a corner shaking, the room was littered with highborn corpses, and Hamlet lay dying in Horatio's arms.<br /><i>"The rest is silence..."</i><br />Horatio, who until that moment no one had noticed was a llama, said,<br /><i>"That's what forgiveness sounds like, screaming and then silence."</i>* Then he bent down and began eating Hamlet's hands.**<br />Christopher Robin whimpered and covered his eyes.<br /><br />Just then, one of the room's tapestries billowed and out stepped Edna St. Vincent Millay. She crossed the room, pausing at the bloody tableau to intone,<br /><i>"Your candle burned at both ends,<br />It gave a lovely light,<br />But for a lack of lithium,<br />You've gone and lost the fight."</i>***<br /><br />She knelt gently beside Christopher Robin, handed him his stuffed bear, and said, <i>"Childhood is the kingdom where nobody dies..."</i><br />A couple of survivors chipped in,<br /><i>"What about Old Yeller?"<br />"And Charlotte the spider? Cried myself silly over that one!"</i><br />Edna St. Vincent Millay quelled them with a look. <i>"As I was saying, childhood is the kingdom where nobody dies. Look around you Christopher Robin. What do you see?"<br />"I see dead people."****<br />"Precisely. And here are all your friends around you safe and sound."</i><br />Pooh gave Christopher Robin an especially reassuring squeeze with his paw. Standing up a little shakily, Christopher Robin said, <i>"I don't like it here! Let's go back to the Hundred Acre Wood."</i><br />So they did.<br /><br />Shortly after entering the wood, just as dusk was stretching shadows into hephalumpine and woozly shapes, they came to a fork in the path which they had never noticed before. It was marked by a signpost. One arrow pointed down one path, reading <i>HELL.</i> Another arrow pointed down the other path, reading <i>ROOM 101.</i> <br /><br />Robert Frost popped up from behind the sign. <br /><i>"Two roads divgered in a yellow wood..."<br />"Get lost, Robert,"</i> growled Edna St. Vincent Millay. <i>"I'm the only poet ex machina in this review!"</i><br /><br />They continued up one of the paths, Pooh humming happily,<br /><i>"The more I win, tiddly pom...</i><br /><br /><br /><b>Winner: Winnie-the-Pooh (Seriously, have you seen Hamlet? He's dead! And are <i> you </i> going to tell Christopher Robin his teddy bear isn't really alive? Well, are you?)</b><br /><br /><br /><br />*Check out Llamas with Hats, especially 1 and 2, on Youtube.<br />**Seriously, Llamas with Hats. <br />***Hamlet is obviously bipolar. Lithium could have helped immensely. Of course one of the common side effects of lithium is tremors, so he probably still wouldn't have won the fight.<br />****An oldie but a goodie. I couldn't resist.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16413254640978596425" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_268049918" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/268049918" />
    <a title="Ambrosia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1811588-ambrosia"><img alt="Ambrosia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386472866p2/1811588.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/268049918?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ambrosia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ambrosia" href="/user/show/1811588-ambrosia">Ambrosia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1811588-ambrosia?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer268049918" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18383590846277564019">At the time of this writing, I am twenty-eight years old. People tell me I come off as intelligent, opinionated, cynical and sarcastic, with a dark and very adult sense of humor. I don't much like children and don't plan to have any of my own. My childhood, while not particularly awful, is nothing I look back on with nostalgia - mostly I'm pretty glad to have gotten to the point where I'm allowed my own life. Generally, my literary tastes run far closer to Patrick Suskind or Neil Gaiman than Mil</span>
  <span id="freeText18383590846277564019" style="display:none">At the time of this writing, I am twenty-eight years old. People tell me I come off as intelligent, opinionated, cynical and sarcastic, with a dark and very adult sense of humor. I don't much like children and don't plan to have any of my own. My childhood, while not particularly awful, is nothing I look back on with nostalgia - mostly I'm pretty glad to have gotten to the point where I'm allowed my own life. Generally, my literary tastes run far closer to Patrick Suskind or Neil Gaiman than Milne.<br /><br />I tell you all this because I want the next statement to have its full impact.<br /><br />The ending of <i>The House At Pooh Corner</i> made me cry like a baby. <br /><br />I know there's a lot of cultural pressure, especially among my demographic, to declare childhood classics like this 'saccharine' and 'condescending'; and, in all fairness, there are some supposedly-classic titles that are indeed worthy of such terms. But I personally didn't find Milne's stories at all saccharine. Rather, I thought them beautifully multilayered, full of observations on the nature of humanity and friendship, as well as being entertaining little tales in their own right. <br /><br />Most importantly, I think they finally illustrated to me what people mean when they talk about 'lost innocence of childhood'. Not that children are pure or good or even particularly innocent (far from it), but more a particular way of looking at the world, where our imaginary friends can be just as important as our real ones, and where the long lazy days stretch out before us like an endless river of sun-dappled adventures; the days when we really <i>don't</i> know what each new day might bring, but that's all right, because it's certain to be new and fascinating.<br /><br />Your mileage may, of course, vary. If these tales don't speak to you, that's fine. Perhaps you truly do find them saccharine and condescending. Perhaps you never had that sense of childhood wonder. Perhaps I'm just delusional.<br /><br />Or perhaps you're simply not old enough for them yet.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18383590846277564019" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer97763541" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer263795716368158741">My first memories of being read aloud to are with this book. My father would read to my sisters and I while my mother completed preparations for supper. We each identified with one of the characters. I was Christopher Robin (being the eldest), my next sister was Pooh (it seemed to me she was always the most interesting character proto-type in all the books I read), my next sister was Rabbit, my next sister was Piglet, and the baby sister was Roo. Our mother was Kanga (of course) and our father w</span>
  <span id="freeText263795716368158741" style="display:none">My first memories of being read aloud to are with this book. My father would read to my sisters and I while my mother completed preparations for supper. We each identified with one of the characters. I was Christopher Robin (being the eldest), my next sister was Pooh (it seemed to me she was always the most interesting character proto-type in all the books I read), my next sister was Rabbit, my next sister was Piglet, and the baby sister was Roo. Our mother was Kanga (of course) and our father was relegated to the role of Eeyore. To this day I remember a passage where Piglet was asleep in the story, at which point we all looked at sister number four and she was fast asleep!<br /><br />All children should have such memories.<br /><br />More recently my teenage daughter and I read aloud several pages from a chapter to a group of pre-school age children attending a library story time. I selected the passage where Pooh attempts to disguise himself as a black cloud by floating to the top of a honey tree by means of hanging on to a balloon. We had the advantage of two readers so one took the voice of Winnie-the-Pooh and the other was the narrator and the voice of Christopher Robin. We acted out Pooh’s think-think-thinking and climbing a tree and falling down the branches and picking stickers from our nose. This was a perfect time to mention to parents some benefits of reading aloud: <br /><br />"Reading to children increases their knowledge of the world, their vocabulary, their familiarity with written language ('book language'), and their interest in reading." <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://childrensbooks.about.com/b/2004/04/22/the-benefits-of-reading-aloud.htm">http://childrensbooks.about.com/b/200...</a> <br /></span>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone who wants to inspire their children</span></div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer34086967" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13187369486839394214">There is no comparison between the original book and the saccharine Disney version of our friend, Pooh. Milne's version is so full of insights into childhood to delight the adult reader that are entirely missed by the more popular version. I bought this book on a whim while trying to start a family, read it to my babies long before they are ready to enjoy these stories, and look forward to sharing Pooh's delightful adventures with them as they grow up. I don't know that I need any other books in</span>
  <span id="freeText13187369486839394214" style="display:none">There is no comparison between the original book and the saccharine Disney version of our friend, Pooh. Milne's version is so full of insights into childhood to delight the adult reader that are entirely missed by the more popular version. I bought this book on a whim while trying to start a family, read it to my babies long before they are ready to enjoy these stories, and look forward to sharing Pooh's delightful adventures with them as they grow up. I don't know that I need any other books in their library ... but of course there will be!! </span>

<div id="review_230297201" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer230297201" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14736947609085233218">Yet another celebrity death match.<br/><br/>(A small windowless room in Elsinore. HAMLET and ROSENCRANZ walk in. Sitting a the table is POOH, a stuffed bear.)<br/><br/>POOH: Can I make a call?<br/>HAMLET: (Standing over POOH:) Who would you like to call?<br/>POOH: My ride. I been here an hour.<br/>HAMLET: Hm. Well, soon as we're through here, we'll get you a ride. Okay?<br/>ROSENCRANZ: (Sitting across from POOH:) Pooh? You own a red Camaro, don't you?<br/>POOH: Yeah.<br/>HAMLET: Do you know Hamlet?<br/>POOH: Yeah, I'm looking at him right now.<br/>HAMLET</span>
  <span id="freeText14736947609085233218" style="display:none">Yet another celebrity death match.<br /><br />(A small windowless room in Elsinore. HAMLET and ROSENCRANZ walk in. Sitting a the table is POOH, a stuffed bear.)<br /><br />POOH: Can I make a call?<br />HAMLET: (Standing over POOH:) Who would you like to call?<br />POOH: My ride. I been here an hour.<br />HAMLET: Hm. Well, soon as we're through here, we'll get you a ride. Okay?<br />ROSENCRANZ: (Sitting across from POOH:) Pooh? You own a red Camaro, don't you?<br />POOH: Yeah.<br />HAMLET: Do you know Hamlet?<br />POOH: Yeah, I'm looking at him right now.<br />HAMLET : No, not me, the old one, my dad. The king. You must have known him.<br />POOH: No.<br />HAMLET: (Pause.) We have a witness, Mr Pooh, who saw you with my dad the night he was murdered<br />POOH: Me? Nope.<br />HAMLET: We have a witness.<br />POOH: I don't care. Don't know him from a stuffed rabbit.<br />ROSENCRANZ: When you say you don't know the man, do you mean that in<br />the philosophical sense, as in "no man really knows another man", or are you referring to the Biblical sense in like, say, Lot knew his wife?<br />POOH: Man, what's he jaggin' on about?<br />ROSENCRANZ: (Slamming hand on table, pointing at POOH:) Look, you're a lying bastard aren't you? You think we're stupid? Huh? Answer me! You think we're stupid?<br />POOH: I don't know. I don't know you.<br />ROSENCRANZ: You don't know me, you don't know him, you don't know old King Hamlet, do you know anybody?<br />POOH: Huh?<br />ROSENCRANZ: (Mocking:) Huh? You're a lying liar, you know that? You're just a liar! You make me sick! (Pointing:) You're charged!<br />POOH: Charged with what?<br />ROSENCRANZ: Charged with what? With being a lying liar, okay? You lying piece of detritus!<br />POOH: I didn't lie. What is detritus?<br />HAMLET: (who is standing authoritatively at a window:) Detective<br />Rosencranz, I'm afraid that we're gonna have to subject Mr.Pooh here to an electrolite neutron-magnetic scan test.<br />POOH: Uh, uh, a what?<br />ROSENCRANZ: Detective HAMLET, I think that's an excellent idea. <br />(ROSENCRANZ gets up and walks toward the door.)<br />ROSENCRANZ: (turning to POOH:) A very good idea. (Opening up door; yells:)<br />WE'RE GONNA NEUTRON THIS LITTLE BASTARD!<br />HAMLET: And I can't wait.<br /><br />(Copy room. HAMLET leafs through papers which read "TRUE" or "FALSE"<br />on one side. He puts them in a particular order and slides them into the paper tray in the copy machine. ROSENCRANZ places a placard on the door which reads "ELECTROLITE NEUTRON-MAGNETIC TEST". ROSENCRANZ walks into the copy room. Over the copy machine is another placard.)<br /><br />ROSENCRANZ: Pooh computes as a part of a loosely formed gang called the Hundred Acre Crips. Think it was them that fucked up your old man?<br />HAMLET: I don't know. (Checking the papers in the machine:) We see what<br />we find out.<br />ROSENCRANZ: Alright, bring him in!<br />(A UNIFORM brings POOH into the room.)<br />HAMLET: Okay Pooh, according to Federal regulation seven dash seven<br />dash b dash point six, I have to inform you that continued exposure to<br />this machine, the electrolite neutron-magnetic test scanner, can be<br />lethal. For your own health, I'm gonna urge you to answer these<br />questions quickly and truthfully. Understand?<br />POOH: Th-that's only a copy machine.<br />HAMLET: Copy machine?<br />POOH: You know, a machine that makes copies.<br />(A knock on the door.)<br />ROSENCRANZ: Hey, Guildenstern.<br />GUILDENSTERN: Hey, your ex-wife's on the phone.<br />HAMLET: Right. I'll get back to her.<br />GUILDENSTERN: (Pointing:) You guys doin' the electrolyte neutron-magnetic test scan? <br />ROSENCRANZ: Yes.<br />GUILDENSTERN: Now, I've asked you guys, I don't even wanna be in the building when that thing's turned on, alright?<br />(GUILDENSTERN shuts the door. HAMLET sighs.)<br />HAMLET: Alright Mr. Pooh, take your right glove off and place your<br />right hand in the designated area. <br />(There is a piece of paper on the machine, with a handprint on it.)<br />HAMLET: Let's go. C'mon, I don't want to waste time with this thing. <br />(ROSENCRANZ puts POOH's hand on the designated area.)<br />HAMLET: Mr Pooh, is your name Winnie the Pooh?<br />POOH : Yes.<br /> (HAMLET pushes the copy button. ROSENCRANZ holds up the answer - TRUE.)<br />HAMLET: So far, so good. (Pushing button again:) Do you belong to a<br />gang known as the Hundred Acre Crips?<br />POOH: Yes.<br />ROSENCRANZ: (Holding up another TRUE result:) Very good. (Patting POOH on the shoulder:) Just keep tellin' the truth, Pooh. Doin' good.<br />HAMLET: You ready?<br />POOH: I don't wanna be gamma-rayed, man!<br />HAMLET: Alright. (Pushing the copy button:) Do you know who killed<br />King Hamlet?<br />POOH: Uh... Uh...<br />HAMLET: Come on.<br />POOH: No!<br />(ROSENCRANZ holds up the scan's result - FALSE.)<br />HAMLET: You can't fool it. It's the space age.<br />ROSENCRANZ: Do it again, and just keep doin' it till he tells the truth. Me, I hafta get outta here because I can't afford to lose any more of my sperm count.<br />POOH: Yo! Wh-wh-what?<br />ROSENCRANZ: I guess we didn't tell you. There's an eleven percent chance of penile stustification.<br />POOH: Penile st-stust -- oh, geez. Okay. Okay, okay. It was Claudius, man. Your friggin uncle. He poisoned him! It was henbane, man!<br />ROSENCRANZ: Henbane.. Weapon of choice of the Nineties. Cheap, legal and you can get it in any apothecary shop.<br />POOH: Am I gonna die? Am I gonna die from these neutron rays?<br />HAMLET: Aw no, man. You're not gonna die from neutron rays because,<br />hm, smart guy, (pointing:) this IS a copy machine. Come on!<br />(HAMLET walks out of the room. ROSENCRANZ puts an arm around POOH and taps him on the cheek, leading him out of the room and laughing as he does so.) <br />HAMLET : Come on, buddy, don't be hard on youself, you ain't the first.<br /></span>

<div id="review_3137403961" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3137403961" />
    <a title="Pamela Shropshire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6535930-pamela-shropshire"><img alt="Pamela Shropshire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420326873p2/6535930.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3137403961?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pamela Shropshire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pamela Shropshire" href="/user/show/6535930-pamela-shropshire">Pamela Shropshire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/858534.The_Complete_Tales_of_Winnie_the_Pooh">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6535930-pamela-shropshire?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6535930-pamela-shropshire?shelf=2020-british-book-club">2020-british-book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6535930-pamela-shropshire?shelf=classics">classics</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3137403961" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13058616304557863746">The January challenge for the British Book Club on Facebook was a Children’s or Young Adult book. I first reread <i>The Wind in the Willows</i>, (which I first read as an adult) then <i>Adam of the Road</i> which was a childhood favorite, and probably the first historical novel I ever read (or perhaps the first was <i>The Witch of Blackbird Pond</i>. While I was replacing them in the grandkids’ reading nook, my eye fell on <i>The Complete Tales of Winnie the Pooh,</i> and I decided to read it, too. <br/><br/>The Pooh stories are one</span>
  <span id="freeText13058616304557863746" style="display:none">The January challenge for the British Book Club on Facebook was a Children’s or Young Adult book. I first reread <i>The Wind in the Willows</i>, (which I first read as an adult) then <i>Adam of the Road</i> which was a childhood favorite, and probably the first historical novel I ever read (or perhaps the first was <i>The Witch of Blackbird Pond</i>. While I was replacing them in the grandkids’ reading nook, my eye fell on <i>The Complete Tales of Winnie the Pooh,</i> and I decided to read it, too. <br /><br />The Pooh stories are one of the classics in children’s literature and for good reason. But they are surprisingly readable for adults as well, from a different perspective. You sort of see the story from the author’s viewpoint, and from this vantage point, the final chapter is particularly poignant. All the animals become aware that Christopher Robin is going away — the implication is that he has reached the age where he is going away to school, and that he is leaving childhood, and his childhood play, behind. <br /><br />Christopher Robin tells Pooh, “I’m not going to do Nothing any more. . . They don’t let you.” And he makes Pooh promise to never forget him, “not even when I’m a hundred.” And then the Closing Sentence, which, being a mom to two boys and having watched them grow up and leave childhood behind, I confess brought a tear to my eyes: <br /><br /><blockquote> But wherever they go, and whatever happens to them on the way, in that enchanted place on the top of the Forest, a little boy and his Bear will always be playing.<br /><br />If I could give it 10 stars, I would.</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="13058616304557863746" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1801584699756642403">The stories themselve were nothing special, the whole book was more character focused and these characters were really funny, also, or especially, for adults. The humor consists mostly of nonsense logic and the characters quirks, like wanting to seem much smarter than they are. That can get tedious after a while, since it is basically the same kind of joke most of the time, but often enough it worked. <br/>They are some details that may get over children's head, but I'm no expert on that. I wouldn't </span>
  <span id="freeText1801584699756642403" style="display:none">The stories themselve were nothing special, the whole book was more character focused and these characters were really funny, also, or especially, for adults. The humor consists mostly of nonsense logic and the characters quirks, like wanting to seem much smarter than they are. That can get tedious after a while, since it is basically the same kind of joke most of the time, but often enough it worked. <br />They are some details that may get over children's head, but I'm no expert on that. I wouldn't want to read that to kids, not because I think it's bad for them in any way, but it would be hard to read out loud because it can be slightly confusing and you would constantly have to break into song. Seriously, does anyone like songs and poems in novels?<br />I liked the creative way the first part was written like Milne was telling his son stories or memories and interacted with him, that was fresh and interesting.<br />On the whole, it was an enjoyable read with the potential to be annoying at times and probably nothing I'd go back to.</span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13108621014714980721">I have no qualms about counting this book towards my Goodreads challenge, not only because of the page count, but because I'm convinced it only gets funnier and more profound the older you get. I can see myself in each of the characters, although there are a couple I particularly identify with (Pooh and Eeyore, depending on the day and my mood ;) ). I was so choked up at the end that I could barely get through the last paragraph. Absolutely wonderful.</span>

          </span>
